<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FEMA_-_58087_-_Photo_by_George_Armstrong_taken_on_07-09-2012_in_Florida.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2848" data-file-width="4288" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/FEMA_-_58087_-_Photo_by_George_Armstrong_taken_on_07-09-2012_in_Florida.jpg/220px-FEMA_-_58087_-_Photo_by_George_Armstrong_taken_on_07-09-2012_in_Florida.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/FEMA_-_58087_-_Photo_by_George_Armstrong_taken_on_07-09-2012_in_Florida.jpg/330px-FEMA_-_58087_-_Photo_by_George_Armstrong_taken_on_07-09-2012_in_Florida.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/FEMA_-_58087_-_Photo_by_George_Armstrong_taken_on_07-09-2012_in_Florida.jpg/440px-FEMA_-_58087_-_Photo_by_George_Armstrong_taken_on_07-09-2012_in_Florida.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FEMA_-_58087_-_Photo_by_George_Armstrong_taken_on_07-09-2012_in_Florida.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Satellite communications after tropical storm Debby in Lake City, Florida, 2012</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Each year, ARC responds to more than 70,000 disasters, including house or apartment fires (making up the majority), hurricanes, floods, earthquakes, tornadoes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hazardous_materials" title="Hazardous materials">hazardous materials</a> spills, transportation accidents, explosions and other natural and man-made disasters.</p>
<p>Although ARC is not a government agency, its authority to provide disaster relief was formalized when, in 1905, it was granted a <a href="/wiki/Congressional_charter" title="Congressional charter">congressional charter</a> to "carry on a system of national and international relief in time of peace and apply the same in mitigating the sufferings caused by pestilence, famine, fire, floods, and other great national calamities, and to devise and carry on measures for preventing the same." The charter is not only a grant of power, but also an imposition of duties and obligations to the nation, to disaster victims and to the people who support its work with their donations.</p>
<p>Disaster relief focuses on emergency disaster-caused needs. When a disaster threatens or strikes, ARC provides <a href="/wiki/Emergency_shelter" title="Emergency shelter">shelter</a>, food and health and <a href="/wiki/Mental_health" title="Mental health">mental health</a> services (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Psychological_First_Aid" title="Psychological First Aid">Psychological First Aid</a>) to address basic human needs. The core of Red Cross disaster relief is assistance to individuals and families to enable them to resume their normal daily activities. The organization provides translation and interpretation when necessary, and maintains a <a href="/wiki/Language_bank" title="Language bank">database of multilingual volunteers</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the local level, ARC chapters operate volunteer-staffed <a href="/wiki/Disaster_Action_Team" title="Disaster Action Team">Disaster Action Teams</a>.</p>
<p>ARC feeds emergency workers of other agencies, handles inquiries from concerned family members outside the disaster area, provides blood and blood products to disaster victims and helps those affected by disaster to access other resources. It is a member of National Voluntary Organizations Active in Disaster (VOAD) and works closely with other agencies such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salvation_Army" title="Salvation Army">Salvation Army</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amateur_Radio_Emergency_Service" title="Amateur Radio Emergency Service">Amateur Radio Emergency Service</a> with whom it has memorandums of understanding.</p>
<p>ARC works to encourage preparedness by providing important literature on readiness. Many chapters also offer free classes to the general public.</p>
<p>A major misconception among the general public is that ARC provides medical facilities, engages in search and rescue operations or deploys ambulances. Instead, first responder roles are left to government agencies as dictated by the <a href="/wiki/National_Response_Framework" title="National Response Framework">National Response Framework</a>. Red Cross societies outside the U.S. may provide such functions; for example, the <i>Cruz Roja Mexicana</i> (Mexican Red Cross) runs a national ambulance service. Furthermore, American Red Cross Emergency Response Vehicles (ERVs) look similar to ambulances. These ERVs instead are designed for bulk distribution of relief supplies, such as meals, drinks and other relief supplies. Although ARC shelters usually assign a nurse to the facility, they are not equipped to provide medical care beyond first aid.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disaster_Services_Workforce">Disaster Services Workforce</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Disaster Services Workforce">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Disaster Services Workforce (DSW) system enrolls volunteers from ARC chapters into a national database of responders, classified by their ability to serve in one or more activities within groups. Services include feeding and sheltering ("mass care") to warehousing, damage assessment, accounting, communications, public affairs and counseling. Responders complete training requirements specific to the services they want to offer, backgrounds, and first aid training.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="National_Response_Framework">National Response Framework</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: National Response Framework">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As a <a href="/wiki/National_Response_Framework" title="National Response Framework">National Response Framework</a> support agency, ARC shelters, feeds and provides other types of emergency relief to victims of disasters. ARC is a co-lead with the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Emergency_Management_Agency" title="Federal Emergency Management Agency">Federal Emergency Management Agency</a> (FEMA) for the mass care portion of Emergency Support Function 6. ARC and FEMA share responsibility for planning and coordinating mass care services with FEMA. ARC has responsibilities for other Emergency Support Functions, such as providing health and mental health services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Disaster_responses">Disaster responses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Disaster responses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Russian_revolution">Russian revolution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Russian revolution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In July 1918 the ARC established a hospital at the entrance of <a href="/wiki/Vladivostok" title="Vladivostok">Vladivostok</a> harbor, and during the year 8 more hospitals. Vladivostok Refugee hospital was opened in early 1919 in former naval barracks and had up to 250 beds. The ARC provided drugs and medical supplies to Russian hospitals during the civil war.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2005_hurricanes">2005 hurricanes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: 2005 hurricanes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FEMA_-_15337_-_Photograph_by_Andrea_Booher_taken_on_09-10-2005_in_Texas.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1803" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/FEMA_-_15337_-_Photograph_by_Andrea_Booher_taken_on_09-10-2005_in_Texas.jpg/220px-FEMA_-_15337_-_Photograph_by_Andrea_Booher_taken_on_09-10-2005_in_Texas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/FEMA_-_15337_-_Photograph_by_Andrea_Booher_taken_on_09-10-2005_in_Texas.jpg/330px-FEMA_-_15337_-_Photograph_by_Andrea_Booher_taken_on_09-10-2005_in_Texas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/FEMA_-_15337_-_Photograph_by_Andrea_Booher_taken_on_09-10-2005_in_Texas.jpg/440px-FEMA_-_15337_-_Photograph_by_Andrea_Booher_taken_on_09-10-2005_in_Texas.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FEMA_-_15337_-_Photograph_by_Andrea_Booher_taken_on_09-10-2005_in_Texas.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Volunteers assist Hurricane victims at the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Houston_Astrodome" title="Houston Astrodome">Houston Astrodome</a>, following Hurricane Katrina.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Forecasting a major disaster before the landfall of <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Katrina" title="Hurricane Katrina">Hurricane Katrina</a>, ARC enlisted 2,000 volunteers to be on a "stand by" deployment list.</p>
<p>According to ARC, during and after hurricanes Katrina, <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Wilma" title="Hurricane Wilma">Wilma</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Rita" title="Hurricane Rita">Rita</a>, they opened 1,470 shelters and registered 3. 8 million overnight stays. 300,000 Red Cross workers (82% unpaid) provided sheltering, casework, communication and assessment services throughout these events. In addition, 346,980 comfort kits (which contain hygiene essentials such as toothpaste, soap, washclothes and toys for children) and 205,360 cleanup kits (containing brooms, mops and bleach) were distributed. The organization served 68 million snacks and meals. Disaster Health services provided 596,810 contacts, and Disaster Mental Health services 826,590 contacts. Emergency financial assistance was provided to 1.4 million families, including 4 million people. Katrina was the first natural disaster in the United States that ARC utilized their "Safe and Well" family location website.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Comair_Flight_5191">Comair Flight 5191</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Comair Flight 5191">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the crash of commuter aircraft <a href="/wiki/Comair_Flight_5191" title="Comair Flight 5191">Comair Flight 5191</a>, the Bluegrass Area Chapter and ARC Critical Response Team (CRT) members were dispatched. This was the worst air disaster within the United States since <a href="/wiki/American_Airlines_Flight_587" title="American Airlines Flight 587">American Airlines Flight 587</a>. Family and Friends reception centers were established near the arrival and departure airports and in Cincinnati, site of the Comair headquarters. Local chapters in <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Alabama" title="Alabama">Alabama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kentucky" title="Kentucky">Kentucky</a> and California provided health and mental health services to family and friends not present in Lexington. Volunteers also staffed the local Emergency Operations Center (EOC) in <a href="/wiki/Lexington,_Kentucky" title="Lexington, Kentucky">Lexington, Kentucky</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="2007_tornadoes">2007 tornadoes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: 2007 tornadoes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:FEMA_-_28112_-_Photograph_by_Mark_Wolfe_taken_on_02-06-2007_in_Florida.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FEMA_-_28112_-_Photograph_by_Mark_Wolfe_taken_on_02-06-2007_in_Florida.jpg/220px-FEMA_-_28112_-_Photograph_by_Mark_Wolfe_taken_on_02-06-2007_in_Florida.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FEMA_-_28112_-_Photograph_by_Mark_Wolfe_taken_on_02-06-2007_in_Florida.jpg/330px-FEMA_-_28112_-_Photograph_by_Mark_Wolfe_taken_on_02-06-2007_in_Florida.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8b/FEMA_-_28112_-_Photograph_by_Mark_Wolfe_taken_on_02-06-2007_in_Florida.jpg/440px-FEMA_-_28112_-_Photograph_by_Mark_Wolfe_taken_on_02-06-2007_in_Florida.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:FEMA_-_28112_-_Photograph_by_Mark_Wolfe_taken_on_02-06-2007_in_Florida.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
ARC is set up in a community hard hit by the tornadoes, Florida, 2007</div>
</div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Florida">Florida</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Florida">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In response to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Florida_Tornado_of_February_2007" title="Central Florida Tornado of February 2007">Central Florida Tornado of February 2007</a>, ARC began a large scale disaster relief operation. At least seven shelters were opened. 40,000 pre-packaged meals were sent by ARC, and across the nation, almost 400 Red Cross volunteers were deployed to assist with local relief efforts. The organization deployed more than 30 Emergency Response Vehicles for community food and supply distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kansas">Kansas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Kansas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>ARC immediately responded to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/May_2007_Tornado_Outbreak" title="May 2007 Tornado Outbreak">May 2007 Tornado Outbreak</a> in central Kansas by setting up emergency shelters for displaced residents and started the distribution of food, water and relief supplies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Minneapolis_Bridge_collapse">Minneapolis Bridge collapse</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Minneapolis Bridge collapse">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/I-35W_Mississippi_River_bridge" title="I-35W Mississippi River bridge">I-35W Mississippi River bridge</a>, the Twin Cities Area Chapter responded with their <a href="/wiki/Disaster_Action_Team" title="Disaster Action Team">Disaster Action Team</a> to provide food, information and comfort. A family service center was set up, and mental health counselors deployed to numerous locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> Donations contributed for this cause totaled US$138,368 and covered the cost of services<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> but not $65,000 in unexpected expenses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Weather and the collapse placed 70% of <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_Minnesota" title="List of counties in Minnesota">Minnesota counties</a> in federal primary or contiguous disaster areas during that August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="International_services">International services</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: International services">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>ARC, as part of the <a href="/wiki/International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement" title="International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement">International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement</a> and its nearly 100 million volunteers, educates and mobilizes communities to overcome life-threatening vulnerabilities. ARC International Services Department focuses on global health, disaster preparedness and response, <a href="/wiki/Restoring_Family_Links" title="Restoring Family Links">Restoring Family Links</a> and international humanitarian law dissemination. ARC is involved with international projects such as the <a href="/wiki/Measles" title="Measles">measles</a> Initiative, <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> programs in Africa, disaster response and relief efforts in response to the <a href="/wiki/2004_Indian_Ocean_earthquake_and_tsunami" title="2004 Indian Ocean earthquake and tsunami">2004 South Asia tsunami</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Disaster_preparedness_and_response">Disaster preparedness and response</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Disaster preparedness and response">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>ARC international disaster response and preparedness programs provide relief and development assistance to millions of people annually who suffer as a result of natural and human-made disasters. To respond quickly and effectively, ARC has pre-positioned emergency relief supplies in three warehouses managed by the International Federation in Dubai, Malaysia and Panama that are used to respond to disasters. An Emergency Response Unit (ERU) is another method with which ARC responds to international emergencies. An ERU is made up of trained personnel and pre-packaged equipment that is crucial in responding to sudden, large-scale disasters and emergencies in remote locations. American Red Cross ERUs specialize in providing emergency relief supplies and IT and Telecommunications for Red Cross response operations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Haiti">Haiti</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Haiti">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On January 12, 2010, a <a href="/wiki/Moment_magnitude_scale" title="Moment magnitude scale">magnitude 7.0 M<sub>w</sub></a> <a href="/wiki/2010_Haiti_earthquake" title="2010 Haiti earthquake">earthquake</a> struck the <a href="/wiki/Haiti" title="Haiti">Haitian</a> coast 10 miles from the capital of <a href="/wiki/Port-au-Prince" title="Port-au-Prince">Port-au-Prince</a>, causing massive damage, more than 200,000 deaths and displacing nearly 2 million people.</p>
<p>As of March 2011, ARC announced it had allocated $314 million for Haiti earthquake relief and recovery. ARC funded recovery projects to provide transitional homes, health services, disaster preparedness, water and sanitation improvements and livelihoods development. It provided funds for school fees for affected families. As of June 2011, ARC had raised approximately $484 million for Haiti relief and recovery efforts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>A series of blistering reports by <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a> and <a href="/wiki/ProPublica" title="ProPublica">ProPublica</a>, however, found that much of the money Americans donated never made it to help people in Haiti and promises to rebuild neighborhoods were never met.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Global_health">Global health</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Global health">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>ARC International Services global health initiatives focus on preventing and combating infectious diseases such as HIV/AIDS and measles on a large scale. Through cost-effective, community-based health interventions, ARC targets people in need and focuses on accessibility and equity of care, community participation and integration with other community development initiatives, such as water and sanitation projects and food and nutrition programs.</p>
<p>An example of ARC International Services health programming is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Measles_Initiative" title="Measles Initiative">Measles Initiative</a>, launched in 2001, as a partnership committed to reducing measles deaths globally. The initiative provides technical and financial support to governments and communities on vaccination campaigns and disease surveillance worldwide. Leading these efforts are ARC, the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Foundation" title="United Nations Foundation">United Nations Foundation</a>, <a href="/wiki/UNICEF" title="UNICEF">UNICEF</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>. The Measles Initiative has supported vaccination campaigns in more than 60 countries, mostly in Africa and Asia. Since 2001, the initiative has helped vaccinate one billion children in more than 60 developing countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The initiative supported the distribution of more than 37 million insecticide-treated mosquito nets for malaria prevention, 81 million doses of de-worming medicine, 95 million doses of polio vaccine, and 186 million doses of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_A" title="Vitamin A">Vitamin A</a>.</p>
<p>In December 2006, ARC became a founding partner of the Malaria No More campaign. The campaign was formed by leading non-governmental organizations to inspire individuals, institutions and organizations in the private sector to support a comprehensive approach to end malaria, a devastating but preventable disease. ARC supported local Red Cross and Red Crescent volunteers in Africa who educated families and communities about malaria prevention and treatment, such as proper and consistent use of insecticide-treated bed nets. ARC provided technical assistance and capacity-building support to its partners in difficult-to-reach communities.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_tracing_requests">International tracing requests</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: International tracing requests">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>ARC handles international tracing requests and searches for families who have been separated. This service attempts to re-establish contact between separated family members. <a href="/wiki/Restoring_Family_Links" title="Restoring Family Links">Restoring Family Links</a> services provides the exchange of hand-written Red Cross Messages between individuals and their relatives who may be refugees or prisoners of war. At any given time, ARC Restoring Family Links program is handling the aftermath of 20–30 wars and conflicts. The worldwide structure of Red Cross and Red Crescent National Societies and the International Committee of the Red Cross make this service possible. When new information from former Soviet Union archives became available in the 1990s, a special unit was created to handle <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">Holocaust</a> tracing services.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="International_humanitarian_law">International humanitarian law</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: International humanitarian law">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>ARC International Services educates the American public about the guiding principles of international humanitarian law (IHL) for conduct in warfare as set forth by the <a href="/wiki/Third_Geneva_Convention" title="Third Geneva Convention">Geneva Conventions of 1949</a>. In doing so, ARC International Services provides support to ARC chapters in their IHL dissemination efforts, offering courses and providing instructor training.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Service_to_the_Armed_Forces">Service to the Armed Forces</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Service to the Armed Forces">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Poster-red-cross-volunteer-for-victory.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="690" data-file-width="450" height="337" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Poster-red-cross-volunteer-for-victory.jpg/220px-Poster-red-cross-volunteer-for-victory.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Poster-red-cross-volunteer-for-victory.jpg/330px-Poster-red-cross-volunteer-for-victory.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/Poster-red-cross-volunteer-for-victory.jpg/440px-Poster-red-cross-volunteer-for-victory.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poster-red-cross-volunteer-for-victory.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A World War II-era poster encouraged American women to volunteer for the Red Cross as part of the war effort.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>ARC provides emergency and non-emergency services to the United States military. The most notable service is emergency family communications, where families can contact the Red Cross to send important family messages (such as a death in the family, or new birth). ARC can also act as a verifying agency.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> The agency operates <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Call_center" title="Call center">call centers</a> to provide these services.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> ARC works closely with other military societies, such as the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_Veterans_Affairs" title="United States Department of Veterans Affairs">Department of Veterans Affairs</a>, to provide other services to service members and their families. ARC is not involved with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prisoners_of_war" title="Prisoners of war">prisoners of war</a>; these are monitored by the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a>, an international body.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Carney_Airfield,_Solomon_Islands,_Red_Cross_girl._August_1944.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="7444" data-file-width="5857" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Carney_Airfield%2C_Solomon_Islands%2C_Red_Cross_girl._August_1944.jpg/180px-Carney_Airfield%2C_Solomon_Islands%2C_Red_Cross_girl._August_1944.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Carney_Airfield%2C_Solomon_Islands%2C_Red_Cross_girl._August_1944.jpg/270px-Carney_Airfield%2C_Solomon_Islands%2C_Red_Cross_girl._August_1944.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Carney_Airfield%2C_Solomon_Islands%2C_Red_Cross_girl._August_1944.jpg/360px-Carney_Airfield%2C_Solomon_Islands%2C_Red_Cross_girl._August_1944.jpg 2x" width="180"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Carney_Airfield,_Solomon_Islands,_Red_Cross_girl._August_1944.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Carney_Airfield" title="Carney Airfield">Carney Airfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Guadalcanal" title="Guadalcanal">Guadalcanal</a>, Solomon Islands Red Cross girl, August 1944</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One criticism of Red Cross services to the military stems from stories about ARC charging troops during the Second World War and <a href="/wiki/Korean_War" title="Korean War">Korean War</a> token fees for "comfort items" such as toothpaste, coffee, donuts, and cigarettes and for off-base food and lodging. The fee suggestion had been made in a letter dated March 1942 from the Secretary of War <a href="/wiki/Henry_L._Stimson" title="Henry L. Stimson">Henry L. Stimson</a> to Norman H. Davis, the chairman of ARC. The suggestion was that Allied soldiers were being charged money so Americans should be charged too so as to "ensure an equitable distribution among all service personnel of Red Cross resources".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The Red Cross adopted the Secretary's suggestion as policy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a June 18, 1945, address to Congress, General <a href="/wiki/Dwight_D._Eisenhower" title="Dwight D. Eisenhower">Dwight D. Eisenhower</a> said of the Red Cross service in World War II, "The Red Cross, with its clubs for recreation, its coffee and doughnuts in the forward areas, its readiness to meet the needs of the well and to help minister to the wounded has often seemed the friendly hand of this nation, reaching across the sea to sustain its fighting men."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> An account of one World War II American Red Cross Girl is recorded in <i>Destination Unknown</i> by Kathleen Cox; her mother, LeOna Cox, was recruited to Red Cross Service by a fellow teacher at <a href="/wiki/Allegheny_College" title="Allegheny College">Allegheny College</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> Another account of an American Red Cross World War II worker is related in letters by Evelyn Merritt Welden, compiled in the book <i>How to Play During a War: A Free Spirit's Life in Letters,</i> by her son, Lynne Whelden.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_1966_-_American_Red_Cross_-_Jennie_Frankel_and_Terrie_Frankel_perform_for_wounded_soldiers.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1378" data-file-width="2687" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_1966_-_American_Red_Cross_-_Jennie_Frankel_and_Terrie_Frankel_perform_for_wounded_soldiers.jpeg/220px-Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_1966_-_American_Red_Cross_-_Jennie_Frankel_and_Terrie_Frankel_perform_for_wounded_soldiers.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_1966_-_American_Red_Cross_-_Jennie_Frankel_and_Terrie_Frankel_perform_for_wounded_soldiers.jpeg/330px-Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_1966_-_American_Red_Cross_-_Jennie_Frankel_and_Terrie_Frankel_perform_for_wounded_soldiers.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_1966_-_American_Red_Cross_-_Jennie_Frankel_and_Terrie_Frankel_perform_for_wounded_soldiers.jpeg/440px-Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_1966_-_American_Red_Cross_-_Jennie_Frankel_and_Terrie_Frankel_perform_for_wounded_soldiers.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_1966_-_American_Red_Cross_-_Jennie_Frankel_and_Terrie_Frankel_perform_for_wounded_soldiers.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Great Lakes Naval Hospital 1966, Jennie and Terrie Frankel perform for wounded soldiers. The frequent visits by the early Doublemint Twins were arranged through the American National Red Cross (see Red Cross letters of thanks to twins, posted on Wikimedia Commons).</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_-_American_Red_Cross_October_24,_1966_Letter_of_thanks_to_Jennie_and_Terrie_Frankel_for_entertaining_wounded_servicemen.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2672" data-file-width="1992" height="295" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_-_American_Red_Cross_October_24%2C_1966_Letter_of_thanks_to_Jennie_and_Terrie_Frankel_for_entertaining_wounded_servicemen.jpg/220px-Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_-_American_Red_Cross_October_24%2C_1966_Letter_of_thanks_to_Jennie_and_Terrie_Frankel_for_entertaining_wounded_servicemen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_-_American_Red_Cross_October_24%2C_1966_Letter_of_thanks_to_Jennie_and_Terrie_Frankel_for_entertaining_wounded_servicemen.jpg/330px-Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_-_American_Red_Cross_October_24%2C_1966_Letter_of_thanks_to_Jennie_and_Terrie_Frankel_for_entertaining_wounded_servicemen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_-_American_Red_Cross_October_24%2C_1966_Letter_of_thanks_to_Jennie_and_Terrie_Frankel_for_entertaining_wounded_servicemen.jpg/440px-Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_-_American_Red_Cross_October_24%2C_1966_Letter_of_thanks_to_Jennie_and_Terrie_Frankel_for_entertaining_wounded_servicemen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Great_Lakes_Naval_Hospital_-_American_Red_Cross_October_24,_1966_Letter_of_thanks_to_Jennie_and_Terrie_Frankel_for_entertaining_wounded_servicemen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Great Lakes Naval Hospital – American Red Cross October 24, 1966 Letter of thanks to Jennie and Terrie Frankel for entertaining wounded servicemen.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Vietnam_War" title="Vietnam War">Vietnam War</a> 627 American women served in the ARC Supplemental Recreation Overseas Program. At the invitation of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Army" title="United States Army">United States Army</a> the "Donut Dollies" provided morale-boosting games to soldiers. Due to the mobility of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UH-1_Iroquois" title="UH-1 Iroquois">UH-1 Iroquois</a>, Vietnam Donut Dollies were able to visit troops in forward operating positions. The 2008 documentary film <i><a href="/wiki/A_Touch_of_Home:_The_Vietnam_War%27s_Red_Cross_Girls" title="A Touch of Home: The Vietnam War's Red Cross Girls">A Touch of Home: The Vietnam War's Red Cross Girls</a></i> tells the story of these women. ARC also provided services to entertain wounded soldiers at the Great Lakes Naval Hospital during the Vietnam War. Twins Jennie and Terrie Frankel, known for their portrayal of the "<a href="/wiki/Doublemint" title="Doublemint">Doublemint Twins</a>," made several visits to the hospital in the late 1960s to perform with their accordions and sing folk songs with their guitar and received letters of thanks from Hospital Field Director, Carolyn F. Norton and Recreation Supervisor, Edna R. Schweitzer.</p>
<p>In 2011, the Service to the Armed Forces (SAF) unit was reorganized and began receiving $24 million per year from Congress for operating expenses. Along with being downsized there was a consolidation into four regional locations (San Diego, CA, Ft. Sill, OK, Louisville, KY, and Springfield, MA). In 2012 the first stories began breaking about long call times and the poor quality of response from call takers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> along with questions about whether money was being used appropriately.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In 2015, the San Diego and Springfield locations were closed because an online option for families was implemented.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Controversies">Controversies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Controversies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Johnson_&amp;_Johnson_suit_over_Red_Cross_image"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Johnson_.26_Johnson_suit_over_Red_Cross_image">Johnson &amp; Johnson suit over Red Cross image</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Johnson &amp; Johnson suit over Red Cross image">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emblems_of_the_Red_Cross#Johnson_.26_Johnson_v._American_Red_Cross" title="Emblems of the Red Cross">Emblems of the Red Cross § Johnson &amp; Johnson v. American Red Cross</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Red_Cross.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Emblems_of_the_International_Red_Cross_and_Red_Crescent_Movement" title="Emblems of the International Red Cross and Red Crescent Movement">red cross</a> flag.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On August 7, 2007,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Johnson_%26_Johnson" title="Johnson &amp; Johnson">Johnson &amp; Johnson</a> (J&amp;J) filed suit against ARC over its sublicensing of the Red Cross image for the production of first aid kits and similar products, which it alleged competed with the company's products. The suit also asked for the destruction of all non-Johnson &amp; Johnson Red Cross Emblem bearing products and demanded that ARC pay <a href="/wiki/Punitive_damages" title="Punitive damages">punitive damages</a> and J&amp;J's legal fees.</p>
<p>The Red Cross' position was that it had licensed its name to first aid kit makers in an effort to encourage readiness for disasters and that license revenues supported humanitarian work.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> J&amp;J claimed that the Red Cross's commercial ventures were outside the scope of historically well-agreed usage and were in direct violation of federal statutes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-jnj_pressrelease_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-jnj_pressrelease-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>On May 14, 2008, a federal judge ruled against J&amp;J.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> In June 2008, the two organizations announced a settlement had been reached allowing both parties to use the symbol.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Court_ordered_consent_decree">Court ordered consent decree</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=39" title="Edit section: Court ordered consent decree">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The FDA took court action against ARC in response to deficiencies in their procedures for ensuring blood supply safety. The resulting consent decree outlines violations of federal law that ARC engaged in before 1993.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> ARC paid millions of dollars in fines.</p>
<p>ARC Biomedical Services instituted a standardized computer system to maintain the blood donor database; five National Testing Laboratories (NTLs) that test some six million units of blood annually;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Drew" title="Charles R. Drew">Charles Drew</a> Biomedical Institute, which provides training and other educational resources to Red Cross Blood Services' personnel; a Quality Assurance/Regulatory Affairs Department, which helps to ensure regulatory compliance; and, a centrally managed blood inventory system.</p>
<p>The Consent Decree was amended in 2003 with penalties for specific violations.</p>
<p>The FDA could impose penalties after April 2003 up to the following maximum amounts:</p>
<ul>
<li>$10,000 per event (and $10,000 per day) for any violation of an ARC standard operating procedure (SOP), the law, or consent decree requirement and timeline</li>
<li>$50,000 for preventable release of each unit of blood for which the FDA finds a reasonable probability of serious adverse health consequences
<ul>
<li>$5,000 for the release of each unit that may cause temporary problems, up to a maximum of $500,000 per event</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>$50,000 for the improper re-release of each unsuitable blood unit that was returned to ARC inventory</li>
<li>$10,000 for each donor inappropriately omitted from the National Donor Deferral Registry, a list of all unsuitable donors</li>
</ul>
<p>The FDA continued to apply pressure and fines to ARC in order to enforce compliance with regulations, including a $1.7 million fine in June 2008.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>ARC worked closely with the FDA to develop a more robust system. The systems resulted in a five-year period of sustained compliance that led to the release from the Consent Decree as of December 4, 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="September_11_controversy">September 11 controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=40" title="Edit section: September 11 controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the aftermath of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/September_11,_2001_attacks" title="September 11, 2001 attacks">September 11, 2001 attacks</a>, ARC and other charitable organizations solicited funds and blood donations. <a href="/wiki/Bernadine_Healy" title="Bernadine Healy">Dr. Bernadine Healy</a>, the president of ARC, appeared on telethons urging individuals to give generously.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, according to America's Blood Centers, the non-profit consortium that provides the other 50% of the United States blood supply, no national blood drive was needed, since localized blood drives in the affected areas would be sufficient to meet the demand. ARC felt that the terrorist attacks were a sign of increased instability and urged people to donate blood, even though it was not needed at that time. In the end, some of the unused blood was destroyed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>ARC created a "Liberty Fund" that was ostensibly designed for relief for victims of the terrorist attacks. However, when the fund was closed in October, after exceeding the goals of donations, only 30% of the $547 million received was spent as the standard disaster relief guidelines for meeting victims needs had been supplied to them. Dr. Healy announced that the majority of the remainder of the money would be used to increase blood supply, improve telecommunications and prepare for future terror attacks.</p>
<p>In February 2002, <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> magazine carried a column claiming that ARC representatives were visiting upscale apartment buildings in wealthy <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> neighborhoods and distributing donated money (up to three months' rent or mortgage payments) to New Yorkers who had been "displaced, traumatized, or merely inconvenienced" by the terrorist attacks, without regard to whether the recipients were in financial need.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>
<p>Many donors felt that they had donated specifically to the victims of the September 11 attacks and objected to Healy's official plan for the diversion of funds. Survivors complained of the bureaucratic process involved in requesting funds and the slow delivery of the checks to meet immediate needs. Congressional hearings were held and New York State Attorney General <a href="/wiki/Eliot_Spitzer" title="Eliot Spitzer">Eliot Spitzer</a> investigated the organization. In the end, ARC appointed former US senator <a href="/wiki/George_J._Mitchell" title="George J. Mitchell">George Mitchell</a> to handle distribution of funds. Healy was forced to resign and the Red Cross pledged that all funds would go to directly benefit attack victims.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup> Healy received a severance payment of $1,569,630.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Blood_donation_controversy">Blood donation controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=41" title="Edit section: Blood donation controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MSM_blood_donor_controversy" title="MSM blood donor controversy">MSM blood donor controversy</a></div>
<p>ARC faced criticism from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lesbian,_gay,_bisexual,_and_transgender" title="Lesbian, gay, bisexual, and transgender">lesbian, gay, bisexual, and transgender</a> (LGBT) advocacy organizations for prohibiting <a href="/wiki/Men_who_have_sex_with_men" title="Men who have sex with men">men who have sex with men</a> (MSM) from donating blood. This policy was an FDA requirement for all blood collection companies and organizations in the United States. Specifically, the FDA instructs blood collection organizations to "defer for 12 months from the most recent sexual contact, a man who has had sex with another man during the past 12 months".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup> Consequently, ARC was legally unable to collect blood from such men. In 2006, along with the <a href="/wiki/AABB" title="AABB">AABB</a> and America's Blood Centers, ARC petitioned the FDA to remove the requirement from blood donations, citing better screening technologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> As of December 2016, the American Red Cross reports on its website that its deferral of MSM from donating blood for 12 months after any sexual contact with another man is aligned with the guidance issued by the U.S. Food and Drug Administration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Political_influence_controversy">Political influence controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=42" title="Edit section: Political influence controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As president, <a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Dole" title="Elizabeth Dole">Elizabeth Dole</a> overruled professional staff and ordered an <a href="/wiki/HIV/AIDS" title="HIV/AIDS">HIV/AIDS</a> prevention manual to be rewritten to make references to homosexuality, premarital sex and condom use more responsive to conservative critics, according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Nation" title="The Nation">The Nation</a></i>, which claimed that she allowed politics to affect many Red Cross policies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hurricane_Katrina_controversy">Hurricane Katrina controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=43" title="Edit section: Hurricane Katrina controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In March 2006, investigations of allegations of fraud and theft by volunteers and contractors within ARC Katrina operations were launched by the <a href="/wiki/Louisiana" title="Louisiana">Louisiana</a> <a href="/wiki/Attorney_general#United_States" title="Attorney general">Attorney-General</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Federal_Bureau_of_Investigation" title="Federal Bureau of Investigation">Federal Bureau of Investigation</a> (FBI).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> In response, ARC increased its internal and external education of the organization's fraud and waste hotline for confidential reporting to a third party agency.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2007)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The organization also elected to implement a background check policy for all volunteers and staff, starting in 2006.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup></p>
<p>In April 2006, an unnamed former ARC official leaked reports made by the <a href="/wiki/International_Committee_of_the_Red_Cross" title="International Committee of the Red Cross">International Committee of the Red Cross</a> and the <a href="/wiki/British_Red_Cross" title="British Red Cross">British Red Cross</a>. Such reports are typical in a large-scale disaster relief operation involving other national Red Cross societies to solicit their input, but are usually confidential and not released to the general public. These particular reports were particularly critical of ARC operations in Katrina-affected regions, although the British Red Cross report strongly praised ARC volunteers in their efforts.</p>
<h3><span id="Storms_controversy_(Hurricane_Sandy,_Isaac,_other_major_storms)"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Storms_controversy_.28Hurricane_Sandy.2C_Isaac.2C_other_major_storms.29">Storms controversy (Hurricane Sandy, Isaac, other major storms)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=44" title="Edit section: Storms controversy (Hurricane Sandy, Isaac, other major storms)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In October 2014, independent public interest news broadcasters <a href="/wiki/NPR" title="NPR">NPR</a> and <a href="/wiki/ProPublica" title="ProPublica">ProPublica</a> published investigative reports on the Red Cross's handling of US East Coast Hurricanes <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Sandy" title="Hurricane Sandy">Sandy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hurricane_Isaac_(2012)" title="Hurricane Isaac (2012)">Isaac</a>, citing internal Red Cross documents and interviews with former Red Cross and government officials. It criticized the organization's response in failing to meet the immediate needs of victims. It also described "an organization so consumed with public relations that it hindered the charity's ability to provide disaster services."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=American_Red_Cross&amp;action=edit&amp;section=45" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<ul>
<li>Dulles, Foster Rhea. <i>The American Red Cross: A History.</i> New York: Harper and Brothers.</li>
<li>Irwin, Julia. <i>Making the World Safe: The American Red Cross and a Nation's Humanitarian Awakening.</i> New York: Oxford University Press, 2013.</li>
<li>Irwin, Julia F., "<a class="external text" href="http://onlinelibrary.wiley.com/doi/10.1111/hoeq.12022/pdf" rel="nofollow">Teaching 'Americanism with a World Perspective': The Junior Red Cross in the U.S. Schools from 1917 to the 1920s</a>". <i>History of Education Quarterly</i> 53 (Aug. 2013), 255–79.</li>
<li>Jones, Marian Moser. <i>The American Red Cross From Clara Barton to the New Deal.</i> Baltimore, MD: Johns Hopkins University Press; 2013.</li>
<li>Whelden, Lynne Merritt. <i>How to Play During a War: A Free Spirit's Life in Letters</i>. Canton, PA: Lynne Whelden Productions; 2007.</li>
</ul>
